    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="5c636ac3950f4656ac44ae6c576bb9c5">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c2912bb7-fc47-4707-94cd-f8e4cdacc1ff'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d131818610" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Porous organic polymers (POPs) as electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are attracting increasing attention. However, the application of porous organic radical polymers (PORPs) with unique properties in SIBs is still in an infancy. Herein, two 3D germanium-based POPs with conjugated diacetylene units (Ge-DA) and acetylene units (Ge-A) are designed and synthesized. Carbon radicals in Ge-DA are verified by electron paramagnetic resonance and the resonance structure is calculated by density functional theory, showing a lower lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy level (−5.46 eV) and a narrow energy gap (ca. 1.64 eV). In contrast, Ge-DA as an anode for SIBs shows superior electrochemical properties to that of Ge-A counterpart, including a higher specific discharge capacity of 349 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, excellent cyclability with a capacity of 112 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> for at least 5000 cycles and high rate performance of 135 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup>, proving that the different electronic structures of the diacetylene and monoacetylene units affect the properties of materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202410372-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202410372-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have emerged as promising alternatives for energy storage systems with the advantages of abundant sodium natural resources, easy exploitation, and low cost.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0001_R_d131818601e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0003_R_d131818601e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Synth.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Over the past few years, an extensive range of electrode materials have been widely developed for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0004_R_d131818601e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater. Technol.">4</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0010_R_d131818601e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, inorganic crystal lattice may result in slow kinetics and unstable structure due to the high lattice evolution energy and a large volume of structural strain. In addition, most inorganic materials originate from non-renewable mineral resources and extremely complex smelting processes could lead to environmental pollution and excessive energy consumption, which runs counter to efforts to develop green and sustainable energy storage systems. In this context, organic electrode materials have received more and more attention because of their resource-rich, environmental, and sustainable feature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0011_R_d131818601e380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Rev.">11</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0014_R_d131818601e380" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Most importantly, the electrochemistry process of organic electrode materials is realized through the conversion or rearrangement of covalent bonds, which is insensitive to the ion size, thus facilitating the uptake and storage of sodium ions with a large radius. Moreover, the best-known structural variability of organic materials can give rise to positive and effective countermeasures to overcome their dissolution in electrolytes and poor electrical conductivity. Porous organic polymers (POPs), as a significant class of organic polymers, have shown more outstanding features in terms of cycling stability and electrochemical properties with the advantages of rigid frameworks, high surface area, and thermal stability. Especially, their designable and flexibly tunable moieties, and rich functionalities could rationally and precisely manipulate the desired features.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0015_R_d131818601e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Mater. Adv.">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0019_R_d131818601e386" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Chem. Synth.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Organic radicals have attracted much attention in battery applications owing to their unique electronic and redox properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0020_R_d131818601e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Pure Appl. Chem.">20</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0023_R_d131818601e393" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Mater.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Organic radicals and open-shell resonance units have been engineered into POPs, which were studied on SIBs in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0024_R_d131818601e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Electrochem. Solid-State Lett.">24</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0027_R_d131818601e398" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specially, open-shell organic materials, also known as radicals, possess one or more unpaired electrons, which could endow them with smaller SOMO-LUMO energy gap and rapid redox kinetics. These unique spintronic features make them potential candidates for good electrical conductivity and electrochemical properties. However, their incompletely satisfied valency may result in the thermodynamically and kinetically unstable structure of open-shell radicals. Steric protection has been demonstrated to be an effective strategy to obtain persistent and stable organic open-shell materials, in which the radicals can be isolated and their interactions are also weakened.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0028_R_d131818601e403" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In a response, organic radicals could be integrated into POPs to construct stable porous organic radical polymers (PORPs). Interestingly, they could be manipulated through bottom-up strategies by elaborately stitching building blocks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0026_R_d131818601e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0029_R_d131818601e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - CCS Chem.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0030_R_d131818601e409" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Alkyny-rich porous materials, such as graphdiyne and its derivatives, usually have the characteristics of <i>sp</i>/<i>sp</i><sup>2</sup> hybrid carbon, special electronic structures, low atomic density, and high affinity for metal atoms, which have shown excellent performance as electrode materials in battery application.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0031_R_d131818601e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">31</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0042_R_d131818601e421" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Acta Phys.-Chim. Sin.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The electronic structures of conjugated multiple bonds have been studied. For example, small-molecule solid compounds containing conjugated diacetylene have been reported to have diradical resonance structures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0043_R_d131818601e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Polym. Sci. Part A: Polym. Chem.">43</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0044_R_d131818601e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Polym. J.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> According to calculations from literatures,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0045_R_d131818601e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Org. Lett.">45</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0046_R_d131818601e432" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Struct. Chem.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the <i>π</i> conjugation of the diyne is not as stable as diene.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0045_R_d131818601e439" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Org. Lett.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the single and the triple bonds in compounds buta-1,3-diyne have double bond character. This led&nbsp;to a possible open-shell resonance structure displayed at certain percentage, which was rationalized by the natural bond orders (BOs) via the natural resonance theory (NRT) method.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0046_R_d131818601e445" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Struct. Chem.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, the diacetylene units were expected to be constructed into 3D organic frameworks applied as anodes to obtain excellent properties for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we selected tetraethynylgermane as a monomer to construct polymers. Germanium atoms have lower ionization energy than carbon and silicon atoms, which may favor more electron delocalization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0047_R_d131818601e453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Lett.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, germanium atoms and conjugated diacetylenes are introduced as nodes and linkers, respectively, to construct POPs materials to enhance their electrochemical properties. A 3D germanium-based porous organic polymer (Ge-POP) Ge-DA with open-shell resonance structure was synthesized by Eglinton homocoupling reaction, and its homological Ge-A was synthesized by salt elimination reaction. Carbon radicals in Ge-DA were confirmed by electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR), which has a comparatively low LUMO energy level (−5.46&nbsp;eV) by density functional theory (DFT), giving a narrow energy gap (ca. 1.64&nbsp;eV). Compared with Ge-A, Ge-DA anodes for SIBs show an excellent cycling stability, higher specific capacity and rate capability, revealing structure-property relationship and demonstrating potential application prospect for Ge-DA in energy storage fields.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202410372-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202410372-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>Based on the open-shell resonance diacetylene units, a Ge-PORP named Ge-DA with Ge (<i>sp<sup>3</sup></i>) as the nodes and conjugated diacetylene (DA) as the linkers was synthesized through Cu(II)-catalyzed Eglinton homocoupling reaction<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0029_R_d131818601e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - CCS Chem.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0048_R_d131818601e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">48</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0049_R_d131818601e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Chem. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0001">1</a>) of tetrahedral tetraethynylgermane monomer<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0047_R_d131818601e482" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Lett.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> according to the literature (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, a new 3D homologous Ge-POP named Ge-A with an acetylene (A) as a linear linker was synthesized via lithiation and subsequent salt elimination reactions<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0047_R_d131818601e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Chem. Lett.">47</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0050_R_d131818601e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Z Naturforsch B">50</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0051_R_d131818601e493" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Organometallics">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> of tetraethynylgermane lithium and tetrachlorosilane. Two Ge-POPs with similar geometries but different structural units were successfully characterized. Experimental and characterization details are depicted in the Supporting Information.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410372-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/2c151b2d-eb72-4e67-b2bb-027ec87828f8/adfm202410372-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/2c151b2d-eb72-4e67-b2bb-027ec87828f8/adfm202410372-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/77afeea1-ec0b-44c6-81dc-22e55171bbb6/adfm202410372-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/2c151b2d-eb72-4e67-b2bb-027ec87828f8/adfm202410372-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410372-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Synthesis and structural diagram of porous organic polymers Ge-DA and Ge-A.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) confirmed the amorphous nature of Ge-DA and Ge-A (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Fourier Transform Infrared (FT-IR) spectra show that the stretching vibration of the terminal C≡C─H bond in Ge-DA disappeared at 3282 cm<sup>−1</sup>, indicating that the homocoupling reaction was completed (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0002">2a</a>). The stretching vibration of the C≡C─H bond in Ge-A becomes weaker due to the existence of small amounts of unreacted terminal alkyne groups. Compared with tetraethynylgermane, the stretching vibration of the C≡C bond in Ge-DA disappeared at 2053 cm<sup>−1</sup> probably due to the formation of the diacetylene moiety (─C≡C─C≡C─).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0030_R_d131818601e534" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The stretching vibration peak of the C≡C bonds at 2053 cm<sup>−1</sup> remains in Ge-A. According to the solid-state <sup>13</sup>C nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectrum of Ge-DA, a broad and high-intensity peak assigned to C(<i>sp<sup>2</sup></i>) at ≈127 ppm was observed due to the amorphous framework and the paramagnetic effect of the open-shell radical material (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0002">2b</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0029_R_d131818601e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - CCS Chem.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0048_R_d131818601e551" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For Ge-A, a broad peak with high intensity at ≈105 ppm is ascribed to C(<i>sp</i>) connected to Ge atoms. The low-intensity peak at ≈79 ppm indicates the presence of unreacted terminal alkynes in Ge-A again. The structure of Ge-DA was further characterized by X-ray Photoelectron Spectroscopy (XPS) analysis. The C 1s peak was divided into three peaks (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). The peaks at 284.74 and 285.57 eV are ascribed to C(<i>sp<sup>2</sup></i>) and C(<i>sp</i>), respectively, and the peak observed at 288.43 eV could be assigned to C─O/C═O as a result of the surface of the materials absorbing oxygen or exposed, uncoupled terminal acetylenic bonds reacting with oxygen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0029_R_d131818601e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - CCS Chem.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0036_R_d131818601e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Adv. Energy Mater.">36</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0037_R_d131818601e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Energy Environ. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0048_R_d131818601e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">48</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0052_R_d131818601e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Chem. Commun.">52</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0053_R_d131818601e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Nat. Commun.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> XPS C 1s spectra of Ge-A show that the peak located at 284.81 eV is attributed to C(<i>sp</i>) bonded to Ge atoms (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The binding energies of Ge 3d peaks for Ge-DA and Ge-A were basically consistent with that of monomer tetraethynylgermane (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). In addition, the signals of Cu 2p in Ge-DA and Li 1s in Ge-A are silent in the XPS analysis (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherm measurements of Ge-DA and Ge-A were both performed at 77 K to investigate their specific surface areas and their porosity properties (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0002">2c</a>). The isotherms of Ge-POPs both display fast uptake under low relative pressures and hysteresis loops, which indicates the concomitance of micropores and mesopores in the frameworks with Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface areas of 368.8 and 389.8 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for Ge-DA and Ge-A, respectively. The corresponding pore sizes calculated by DFT were 1.98 and 4.22 nm for Ge-DA, 1.19 and 4.22 nm for Ge-A. From the UV–vis-diffuse reflectance (UV–vis-DR) spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0002">2d</a>), it can be seen that Ge-DA has a stronger absorbance and a wider absorption wavelength range (from 200 to 2200 nm) compared to Ge-A. Thermogravimetric plots reveal that Ge-DA and Ge-A have good thermal stability and could be stable up to ≈220&nbsp;°C under the air atmosphere (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). The micromorphologies of Ge-DA and Ge-A are irregular spherical particles and amorphous particles presented by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Both carbon and germanium are uniformly distributed in Ge-DA and Ge-A, respectively, according to the TEM mapping images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410372-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/016739fb-d99d-475d-8275-d39214853e1b/adfm202410372-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/016739fb-d99d-475d-8275-d39214853e1b/adfm202410372-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/66f0e424-12a5-406e-a77a-981ca1aff8eb/adfm202410372-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/016739fb-d99d-475d-8275-d39214853e1b/adfm202410372-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410372-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) FT-IR spectra of tetraethynylgermane, Ge-DA and Ge-A. The solid-state <sup>13</sup>C MAS NMR spectra b), N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms (77 K) and the pore size distributions c), and UV–vis-DR absorption spectra d) of Ge-DA and Ge-A.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To investigate the feature of radical species in Ge-based POPs, Ge-DA and Ge-A powder in sealed quartz tubes were measured by solid-state electron paramagnetic resonance (EPR) under ambient conditions. The signal of Ge-A is silent, whereas Ge-DA presents a very strong EPR signal at g-factor ≈2.004 (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0003">3a</a>), which indicates the presence of carbon-centered radicals.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0026_R_d131818601e643" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The optical band gap of Ge-DA has been calculated to be 1.12 eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0003">3b</a>), narrower than that of Ge-A (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information), which was very likely due to the open-shell structure and their spatial interactions. To further investigate the radical nature of Ge-DA, a representative fragment of Ge-DA was optimized, whose spin density distribution and the geometries were calculated by DFT. Reported works<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0029_R_d131818601e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - CCS Chem.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0044_R_d131818601e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Polym. J.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggest that the conjugated diacetylene unit has an open-shell diradical resonance structure. The optimized non-radical and diradical geometries were calculated and shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0003">3c</a>. For the representative fragment of Ge-DA with open-shell diradical resonance, the singly occupied molecular orbital (SOMO, <i>β</i>) (−7.10&nbsp;eV) and LUMO (−5.46&nbsp;eV) energy levels generate a narrower energy gap of 1.64&nbsp;eV, which means higher electronic conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0054_R_d131818601e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Energy Storage Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the optimized open-shell diradical resonance structure, the intense spin density focuses on C atoms adjacent to Ge atoms on the backbone (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0003">3d</a>). In the radical geometry, the length of the C≡C bond is 1.285 Å, which is longer than that of original non-radical geometry (1.199 Å). Furthermore, the original length of the C─C bond decreased from 1.332 to 1.277 Å (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0003">3d</a>). These results indicate double bond characteristics in the open-shell structure of Ge-DA.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410372-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9bd6afc3-0960-40b8-83f4-ae0eda9f1713/adfm202410372-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9bd6afc3-0960-40b8-83f4-ae0eda9f1713/adfm202410372-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2dc0ae0c-739e-40c0-a35f-7c882e6b2421/adfm202410372-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9bd6afc3-0960-40b8-83f4-ae0eda9f1713/adfm202410372-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410372-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) EPR spectra of Ge-DA and Ge-A at room temperature. b) Calculated optical band gap of Ge-DA from the UV–vis-DR absorption. c) Calculated LUMOs and HOMO/SOMO (β) of optimized non-radical (up, singlet) and diradical (bottom, triplet) representative fragments of Ge-DA. d) Calculated spin density and the optimized non-radical (singlet, left) and diradical (triplet, right) geometries of DA with the relevant bond length (Å).</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The unique 3D Ge-DA with an open-shell resonance radical framework, permanent porosity, and low energy gap promotes us to investigate its potential application for SIBs. Consequently, the electrochemical performance of Ge-DA as an anode material for SIBs was investigated in comparison with non-radical POP Ge-A. First, Ge-DA as an anode for SIBs, the cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves were studied at a scan rate of 0.2 mV s<sup>−1</sup> with a potential range of 0.01–2.8 V (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0004">4a</a>). It can be observed that there is a broad cathodic peak in the range of 0.11–0.42 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) from the first sweep, but the reduction peak disappears in the next two cycles, which is associated with electrolyte degradation and the formation of a solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0055_R_d131818601e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">55</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0056_R_d131818601e710" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and certain irreversible side reactions. Sodiation on the polymer skeleton is responsible for further cathodic peaks at 0.01 and 0.05 V. Accordingly, the anodic peak at 2.45 V could be attributed to the process of desodiation. The well overlapping CV curves indicate the excellent reversibility of the Ge-DA anode for SIBs in subsequent scan cycles. The galvanostatic charging and discharging curves of the Ge-DA anode at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0004">4b</a>, which is in line with the CV results. High initial discharge/charge capacities of 547 and 285 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> were shown, achieving 52.1% of the initial Coulombic efficiency due to the formation of the SEI layer. Interestingly, the discharge capacity (312 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) of the Ge-DA anode was improved after 31 cycles of activation. Furthermore, the cycling performance of Ge-DA and Ge-A at high current densities of 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0004">4c</a>) and 1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) are shown, respectively. With gradual activation, Ge-DA anode achieves a discharge/charge capacity of 349 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, which is higher than Ge-A anode (99 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) and some alkyne-rich carbon materials, organic and inorganic materials (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). The specific discharge capacity of Ge-DA anode is on the rise because the electrode material is closer contact with the electrolyte, whose high specific surface area and porosity provide more space for sodiation and expose more active sites for boosting sodium storage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0014_R_d131818601e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0057_R_d131818601e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">57</a>-<a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0059_R_d131818601e746" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Ge-DA anode shows desirable rate performance, delivering discharge capacities of 298, 284, 260, 241, 224, 208, 182, 160, and 135 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at different current densities of 0.05, 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, 5.0, 10.0, and 20.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0004">4d</a>). Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) profiles display charge transfer resistances (Rct) of 55 and 93 Ω<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0060_R_d131818601e759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Nat. Commun.">60</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0061_R_d131818601e759" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> for Ge-DA and Ge-A anodes in SIBs, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The lower Rct of Ge-DA indicates better electrical conductivity. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0004">4e</a> shows the long term cycling stability of Ge-DA at a higher current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>. Surprisingly, even after 5000 cycles, the capacity can still be 112 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a Columbic efficiency of ≈100%, which indicates an excellent cycling stability for long-term Na<sup>+</sup> storage.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410372-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0474b044-478e-4749-86c2-e69ae53c1e46/adfm202410372-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0474b044-478e-4749-86c2-e69ae53c1e46/adfm202410372-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/30b35ac4-71a5-4b13-a632-7e7b17076b59/adfm202410372-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0474b044-478e-4749-86c2-e69ae53c1e46/adfm202410372-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410372-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Electrochemical performance of Ge-DA and Ge-A. a) The first three CV cycles of Ge-DA at 0.2&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Charging/discharging curves of Ge-DA at 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup> versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. c) Comparison of cycling performance of Ge-DA and Ge-A at 200 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Rate behavior of Ge-DA and Ge-A at various current densities. e) Long-term cycling stability for Ge-DA at 5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>CV curves at different scan rates were studied to further investigate the electrochemical kinetic behavior of the Ge-DA anode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15a</a>, Supporting Information). A power-law relationship (<i>i</i> = a<i>ν</i><sup>b</sup>) exists between the peak current (<i>i</i>) and the sampling rate (<i>ν</i>), and the value of b is used as a standard to determine the diffusional (b = 0.5) or capacitive (b = 1.0) behavior of Na<sup>+</sup> storage during the redox process. For Ge-DA anode, the b values of the cathodic and anodic peaks are 0.9527 and 0.7940, respectively, indicating capacitive-and diffusion-dominated hybrid behavior<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0062_R_d131818601e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Nano Lett.">62</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0063_R_d131818601e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - eScience">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15b</a>, Supporting Information). Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15c</a> (Supporting Information) shows the ratio of diffusion controlled and capacitive behavior at a scan rate of 0.8 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. In addition, the capacitive contribution increased from 71.43% to 81.46% with increasing scan rates (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15d</a>, Supporting Information), indicating that the storage dynamics behavior of Na ions in Ge-DA framework is mainly dominated by capacitive-controlled behavior. To investigate the mechanism of storing Na<sup>+</sup>, ex situ EPR was performed on the Ge-DA anode in different states (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0005">5a</a>). The initial material shows a stronger EPR signal with g value ≈2.004. When discharging to 0.01 V, the EPR signal of Ge-DA decreases due to the acceptance of electrons and Na ions in multiple carbon bonds. After charging to 2.8 V, the intensity of the EPR signal returns, indicating that the sodiation and desodiation conversion processes are reversible. Furthermore, the in situ Raman spectroscopy was conducted during the discharging and charging process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). During discharging, the peaks of metal Na with the conjugated diyne bonds become stronger as sodiation processed.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0064_R_d131818601e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">64</a>, <a href="#adfm202410372-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202410372-bib-0065_R_d131818601e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Chem. Commun.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0005">5b</a> shows the binding energies of the representative fragments of Ge-DA and Ge-DA radical with different numbers of Na ions, respectively. The Ge-DA radicals with two or four Na ions both show lower binding energies, which indicates that spin single electrons promote sodiation processes. To further understand the transformation of radicals, the optimized geometries and the electronic spin density distributions of Ge-DA radicals were calculated for different sodiation processes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202410372-fig-0005">5c,d</a>). The initial Na<sub>0</sub>-(Ge-DA radical) shows high spin density mostly around C atoms neighboring Ge atoms and the sodiated Na<sub>2</sub>-(Ge-DA radical) displays intense spin density around two C atoms in the middle, however, the sodiated Na<sub>4</sub>-(Ge-DA radical) did not show any spin density.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202410372-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ce7876a2-55e1-49fa-8ee4-3d0f5f66efaa/adfm202410372-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/ce7876a2-55e1-49fa-8ee4-3d0f5f66efaa/adfm202410372-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a342e4f0-d16e-41ab-9da9-2bd1b6d35e53/adfm202410372-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ce7876a2-55e1-49fa-8ee4-3d0f5f66efaa/adfm202410372-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202410372-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202410372" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ EPR spectra of Ge-DA in three different states. b) The binding energies of the sodiated Ge-DA fragment and Ge-DA radical fragment calculated by DFT. c) Optimized geometries, and d) the spin density distributions of different sodiated Ge-DA radical fragments.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202410372-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202410372-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, two 3D germanium-POPs with open-shell resonance conjugated diacetylene (DA) and non-open-shell alkynyl (A) as linkers, respectively, were designed and synthesized. The carbon radicals of Ge-DA were confirmed by EPR and the open-shell resonance structure was calculated by DFT. The amorphous Ge-POPs have good stability, high specific surface areas, multiple carbon bonds as active sites, and low electrolyte solubility. Benefiting from the open-shell resonance conjugated diacetylene structure, 3D Ge-DA as an anode for SIBs exhibits much better capacity than that of Ge-A, high rate performance, and excellent cycling stability, which proves a close relationship between the electronic structures and the performance of the materials. Overall, our research will widen the horizons for designing radical polymers with promising applications in energy storage.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
